[2025-03-14 09:09] Nicholas Wright: That’s always the challenge. In uncertainty modeling, we try to quantify that “wish”—expressing the probability of recovery given interventions.

[2025-05-20 08:01] Michael Flores: Oh, so the committee asked for something more beyond your main portfolio?
[2025-05-20 08:01] Nicholas Wright: Yes, they want a demonstration of practical application—how my uncertainty modeling connects to real-world outcomes.
[2025-05-20 08:02] Michael Flores: That sounds like a challenge, but honestly, it’s where your expertise really shines. Are you focusing on any particular field for the examples?
[2025-05-20 08:03] Nicholas Wright: I considered using clinical decision-making as a case study, actually. The way practitioners handle ambiguous data is fascinating.
[2025-05-20 08:03] Michael Flores: I’m biased, but I think that’s a great choice. There’s so much gray area in patient adherence and risk prediction.
[2025-05-20 08:04] Nicholas Wright: Exactly. For instance, measuring medication adherence is a perfect illustration of subjective probabilities in practice.
[2025-05-20 08:05] Michael Flores: Definitely. Patients’ self-reports, refill histories, even digital pill bottles—none of them tell the whole story.
[2025-05-20 08:05] Nicholas Wright: Do you have examples where uncertainty made a big difference in patient outcomes?
[2025-05-20 08:06] Michael Flores: Actually, yes. We had a case with an elderly diabetic patient. The EMR showed perfect adherence, but her A1C kept rising. Turned out she misunderstood the dosing schedule.
[2025-05-20 08:07] Nicholas Wright: That’s a classic “apparent certainty masking real uncertainty.” The system says she’s adherent, but reality is more complex.
[2025-05-20 08:08] Michael Flores: Exactly. It’s why I’m interested in integrating behavioral data—context can add nuance to raw numbers.
[2025-05-20 08:08] Nicholas Wright: If I could model that kind of context—maybe with fuzzy logic or possibility theory—it could make AI predictions more robust.
[2025-05-20 08:09] Michael Flores: Possibility theory? I’ve heard the term, but I’m not sure I understand how it differs from probability.
[2025-05-20 08:10] Nicholas Wright: In short, probability quantifies likelihood, while possibility theory ranks plausibility when you lack enough data for true probabilities.
[2025-05-20 08:10] Michael Flores: So it’s a way of formalizing “educated guesses” when data is incomplete?
[2025-05-20 08:11] Nicholas Wright: Yes, and it’s especially useful when expert intuition is all you have. For your patient, the nurse’s gut feeling might carry valuable information.
[2025-05-20 08:12] Michael Flores: That’s fascinating. In practice, we rely on that intuition a lot—especially when labs and logs don’t add up.
[2025-05-20 08:12] Nicholas Wright: It’s a challenge to get AI systems to value those “soft signals” without overfitting.
[2025-05-20 08:13] Michael Flores: Have you considered collaborating with clinicians for your paper? Real-world examples might strengthen your argument for the committee.
[2025-05-20 08:14] Nicholas Wright: That’s a good idea. Would you be open to sharing a few anonymized cases or challenges you’ve seen?
